â”‚ Template file analysis reveals inconsistency: Some templates are fully structured (codebase-analysis.json.tmpl has detailed schema with patterns[], dependencies{}, etc.), but many are placeholders with just {"summary": "", "details": []}. â”‚ Two implementation approaches emerged: Option A (simple display) just cats the template file with syntax highlighting, Option B (rendered display) uses template-renderer.sh to populate mustache variables with sample data. Option A is better because: 1) Shows exact structure agents need to produce, 2) Mustache variables like {{feature_name}} are self-documenting, 3) No risk of sample data being misleading, 4) Simpler with no dependencies.   â”‚

â”‚ Implementation scope decision: Should we modify just fetch-phase.sh, just fetch-phase-details.sh, or both? â”‚ Edge case consideration: What happens when outputs[].schema is null, missing, or points to a non-existent file? The code must gracefully handle these cases. If schema field is missing/null, skip template display entirely. If schema exists but file not found, show a warning like "(Template file not found: path/to/template.tmpl)" so users know templates need to be created. This diagnostic feedback is valuable for template system maintenance.   â”‚

â”‚ Multiple outputs consideration: Phase YAML files can have multiple outputs (outputs[0], outputs[1], etc.). The current code only shows outputs[0]. When adding template display, we should handle ALL outputs in the array, not just the first one. This means iterating through outputs[] and showing template for each one that has a schema field. This ensures complete information for agents working on phases with multiple deliverables.   â”‚

â”‚ Syntax highlighting strategy: Template files have different extensions (.json.tmpl, .md.tmpl, .yaml.tmpl). When displaying in code fences, we should detect the base file type and use appropriate syntax highlighting. For .json.tmpl use ```json, for .md.tmpl use ```markdown, for .yaml.tmpl use ```yaml. This makes templates more readable for agents. The file extension detection logic: strip .tmpl suffix, then check remaining extension.   â”‚

â”‚ Final synthesis: The enhancement requires modifying fetch-phase-details.sh (primary) and fetch-phase.sh (secondary) to extract outputs[].schema from phase YAML, read template files from output_templates/, and display them in code fences with appropriate syntax highlighting. Key implementation points: 1) Use jq to extract schema field for each output, 2) Check template file exists before displaying, 3) Detect file type for syntax highlighting (.json.tmplâ†’```json, .md.tmplâ†’```markdown), 4) Handle missing schemas gracefully, 5) Support multiple outputs per phase. This provides agents with complete structural guidance, reducing ambiguity and increasing output consistency across workflow executions.   â”‚

â”‚ Bonus consideration: This enhancement also serves as a template validation mechanism. By displaying templates when phases are fetched, we'll immediately discover which templates are incomplete placeholders vs fully structured schemas. This creates a feedback loop for template improvement - as workflows are used, gaps become visible. We should document this benefit in the implementation notes so future maintainers understand the diagnostic value of template display beyond just agent guidance.   â”‚
